    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="782c953eceb34f4dbd540fddd22b0721"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="782c953eceb34f4dbd540fddd22b0721">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e408af72-6c84-4cac-81d5-ceded37f6d68'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d80179486" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A solid-solution strategy is proposed to eliminate the impurity in Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> materials. The as-prepared pure-phase Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibits enhanced specific capacity and cycling performance up to 14000 cycles. And a two-phase transition during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion is distinctly revealed, thus providing new insights into both synthesis strategy and ion-storage mechanism of the promising Fe-based mixed-anion-group compound.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f388a9e4-09a2-478b-8031-c5c758278742/anie202316957-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f388a9e4-09a2-478b-8031-c5c758278742/anie202316957-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a25290a8-6cf6-4a49-b762-28269228f9eb/anie202316957-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f388a9e4-09a2-478b-8031-c5c758278742/anie202316957-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d80179888" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Mixed-anion-group Fe-based phosphate materials, such as Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, have emerged as promising cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, the synthesis of pure-phase material has remained a challenge, and the phase evolution during sodium (de)intercalation is debating as well. Herein, a solid-solution strategy is proposed to partition Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> into 2NaFePO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> from the angle of molecular composition. Via regulating the starting ratio of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> during the synthesis process, the nonstoichiometric pure-phase material could be successfully synthesized within a narrow NaFePO<sub>4</sub> content between 1.6 and 1.2. Furthermore, the proposed synthesis strategy demonstrates strong applicability that helps to address the impurity issue of Na<sub>4</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and nonstoichiometric Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> are evidenced to be the pure phase. The model Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode (the content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> equals 1.4) demonstrates exceptional sodium storage performances, including ultrahigh rate capability under 100 C and ultralong cycle life over 14000 cycles. Furthermore, combined measurements of ex situ nuclear magnetic resonance, in situ synchrotron radiation diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy clearly reveal a two-phase transition during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion, which provides a new insight into the ionic storage process for such kind of mixed-anion-group Fe-based phosphate materials and pave the way for the development of high-power sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202316957-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202316957-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The foremost driver behind the rapid progress of rechargeable batteries in recent times is largely attributed to the innovative advancements in electrode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0001_R_d80179476e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Inspired by the great success of LiFePO<sub>4</sub> as the cathode for lithium-ion batteries used in electric vehicles, many researchers have devoted extensive attention to exploring Fe-based phosphate materials for low-cost sodium-ion batteries (SIBs),<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0004_R_d80179476e752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nano Energy">2</a></sup></span> because of their robust crystal structure facilitating rapid and stable Na<sup>+</sup> transportation. In comparison with NASICON-type vanadium-based compounds,<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0005_R_d80179476e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> ion-based materials are characterized as abundant resources and non-toxic, more beneficial to future scale production.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0009_R_d80179476e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A">4</a></sup></span> Notable examples include maricite-NaFePO<sub>4</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0010_R_d80179476e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span> triphylite-NaFePO<sub>4</sub>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0011_R_d80179476e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F,<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0014_R_d80179476e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem.">7</a></sup></span> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0015_R_d80179476e788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> One particular mixed-anion-group compound, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, has been drawing more interest lately because of its outstanding electrochemical activity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0016_R_d80179476e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> As presented in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0001">1a</a>, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is composed of a PO<sub>4</sub>-P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> layered structure. Within the layers, the PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron and FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedron form double-chain structures via corner-sharing, while different layers interconnect by the P-O-P bonding of the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> group.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0015_R_d80179476e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0019_R_d80179476e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span> So, Na<sup>+</sup> ions could transfer along the [010] and [001] directions between the different layers with the results of high ionic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0010_R_d80179476e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a></sup></span> The successful synthesis of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> as a cathode material for SIBs was accomplished by Kim et al., who achieved a reversible capacity of 105.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 C.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0019_R_d80179476e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span> Then, the air stability and all-climate properties were explored by Chou's team with a discharge capacity of 84.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C even at −20 °C.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0020_R_d80179476e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202316957-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/12d08f68-5e7e-4a09-880b-43fb5354178d/anie202316957-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/12d08f68-5e7e-4a09-880b-43fb5354178d/anie202316957-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f45485c7-9970-48f2-a34a-ebbeac29ec8d/anie202316957-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/12d08f68-5e7e-4a09-880b-43fb5354178d/anie202316957-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202316957-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202316957" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic crystal structure of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP; (b) X-ray diffraction patterns and enlarged X-ray diffraction patterns from 32° to 35.5° for different contents; (c) Rietveld refinement of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP; (d) HRTEM image of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Even though Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> has been identified as a promising cathode material for SIBs in prior studies, its preparation of pure-phase material has been a significant obstacle due to the presence of a minor NaFePO<sub>4</sub> impurity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0019_R_d80179476e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0021_R_d80179476e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> The reason for the challenge in obtaining a pure phase material is due to the complex structure of the compound, which includes mixed anions and requires precise control of synthesis conditions. Due to the inferior properties of NaFePO<sub>4</sub>, the storage performance of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is believed to be underestimated.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0012_R_d80179476e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - Chem. Mater.">6a</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0024_R_d80179476e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span> Recently, Cao et al. successfully synthesized phase-pure Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub><i>x</i></sub>P<sub>4</sub>O<sub>12+<i>x</i></sub> via regulating <i>x</i> with large improved long-cycle stability over 10,000 cycles.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0025_R_d80179476e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Besides the issue from the synthesis, the phase evolution during sodium (de)intercalation is questionable. So far, both Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.91</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> exhibit a solid-solution reaction during the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0020_R_d80179476e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0021_R_d80179476e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0026_R_d80179476e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14a - Nano Energy">14a</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0028_R_d80179476e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a></sup></span> This seems contrary to the plateau behavior observed in the charge-discharge profile, which is a typical feature of the two-phase mechanism seen in LiFePO<sub>4</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0029_R_d80179476e999" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></sup></span> This phenomenon could be attributed to the limitations of in-house XRD, which may have lower precision. Furthermore, the energy density of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> remains inconclusive due to its relatively low working potential of approximately 3.0 V.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0019_R_d80179476e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span> Thick electrode processing is an effective approach to increasing energy output by increasing the mass loadings and ratios of active materials while reducing the amount of electrochemically inactive components, such as current collectors (Cu and Al foils), separators, and electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0030_R_d80179476e1018" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">17</a></sup></span> However, achieving high-mass-loading thick electrodes without compromising high-rate performance is a challenging task due to the possibility of cracks or holes during electrode drying.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0031_R_d80179476e1022" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we utilize the molecular design strategy of Li-rich compound <i>x</i>Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub> ⋅ (1-<i>x</i>)LiMO<sub>2</sub> to partition Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> into 2NaFePO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0034_R_d80179476e1059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span> Through adjusting the content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> from 2 to 1, we successfully synthesize a series of pure-phase nonstoichiometric materials in the NaFePO<sub>4</sub> content region between 1.6 and 1.2. The proposed synthesis strategy can also help to address the impurity issue of Na<sub>4</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and nonstoichiometric Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> are found to be the pure phase. The selected pure-phase Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> demonstrates an improved specific capacity of 110.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, excellent rate capability of 50.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 C, and ultralong lifespan of more than 14000 cycles with retention of 83.1 % and nearly Coulombic efficiency of 100 %, superior to the comparative materials with impurity. Additionally, thick Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> electrodes are successfully fabricated with high mass loadings exceeding 40.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and areal capacities of approximately 4.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, showcasing the potential for high-energy-density SIBs. More importantly, via combined investigations of in situ synchrotron-based X-ray diffraction, X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), we reveal that Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode experiences a two-phase transition during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion, which is different from the previously reported solid-solution process. Such findings not only provide new insight into this kind of mixed-anion-group materials but also contribute to future industrial applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202316957-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202316957-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202316957-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202316957-sec-0003-title"> Material design strategy and physical property</h3>
            
            <p>As we all know, there is an ongoing debate about Li-rich compounds, which can be expressed in two different forms: solid-solution Li[Ni<sub><i>x</i></sub>Li<sub>(1-2<i>x</i>)/3</sub>Mn<sub>(2-<i>x</i>)/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> or two-phase mixing <i>x</i>Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub> ⋅ (1-<i>x</i>)LiMO<sub>2</sub>, in the past decades.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0034_R_d80179476e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources">19</a></sup></span> By taking advantage of the experience from Li-rich compounds, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> may be recognized from a new angle of the solid solution of two NaFePO<sub>4</sub> and one Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. Since NaFePO<sub>4</sub> is reported as a major impurity and influences the electrochemical performance greatly, a possible solution to obtain a pure phase is to reduce the amount of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> at the beginning of synthesis. Thus, the content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> in Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is adjusted from 2 to 1. As shown in Figure S1 and Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0001">1b</a>, when the content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> is decreased to 1.6, the characteristic reflections of maricite-NaFePO<sub>4</sub> with orthorhombic structure (space group: <i>Pmnb</i>) at 32.8°, 33.1°, and 34.8° disappear, and the refelction at 10.7° belonging to triclinic Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (space group: <i>P-1</i>) gradually increases when NaFePO<sub>4</sub> is less than 1.2. These findings indicate that the mixed-anion-group compound may form a pure phase with a nonstoichiometric molecular formula when the content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> is in the region between 1.6 and 1.2. To confirm the crystal structure of this nonstoichiometric material, we selected the Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (hereafter denoted as Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP) as the model material and performed X-ray diffraction (XRD) in conjunction with Rietveld refinement. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0001">1c</a>, all the reflections can be indexed to orthorhombic structures with the <i>Pn2<sub>1</sub>a</i> symmetry. The refinement yields lattice parameters of <i>a</i>=17.998(2) Å, <i>b</i>=6.539(1) Å, and <i>c</i>=10.665(1) Å, which are consistent with the previously reported value.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0019_R_d80179476e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a></sup></span> The atom occupancy is listed in Table S1, and it is noted that Fe vacancy exists as a consequence of the nonstoichiometric formula, corresponding well with the ICP result (Table S2). Scanning electron microscope (SEM) image, as shown in Figures S2a and S2b, suggests the high-temperature sintering sample crystallizes in the bulk particles ranging from hundreds of nanometers to dozens of microns in size, and the elements Na, Fe, P, O, and C coexist and are distributed uniformly as shown in the SEM-EDS mapping (Figure S2c). Further analysis using transmission electron microscopy (TEM) confirms the composite nature of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP particles and graphitized carbon. As depicted in Figure S3, well-crystallized Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP nanoparticles with a size of approximately 500 nm are embedded within the graphitized carbon matrix. Additionally, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0001">1d</a>, a thin layer of carbon, measuring approximately 3 nm in thickness, is found to be coated on the surface of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP particles. The lattice fringe of 0.53 nm observed within the nanoparticles is consistent with the interplanar spacing of (210) plane. In addition, Na<sub>3.1</sub>Fe<sub>2.1</sub>PP and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>PP exhibit similar morphology and carbon layer thickness to Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP, as shown in Figures S4 and S5. The high-resolution XPS spectrum of Fe, depicted in Figure S6, exhibited a double-peak feature with noticeable asymmetry, with binding energies situated at 711.7 and 725.9 eV, respectively, representing Fe<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0026_R_d80179476e1320" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14a - Nano Energy">14a</a></sup></span> This indicates that there is no change in the valence of Fe even in the nonstoichiometric compound. Subsequently, as shown in Figure S7, Raman spectroscopy reveals that the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio for the Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP sample is 0.75, indicating that the compositing carbon possesses a moderate level of structural disorder.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0035_R_d80179476e1332" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span> Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was also conducted to investigate the structural characteristics of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP. As shown in Figure S8, the intensive peaks located at 545 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the FT-IR spectra are ascribed to the vibration of Fe-O bond in the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. Several peaks in the ranges of 400–700 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 975–1300 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the O-P-O bending and P-O stretching vibrations of the PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra, while peak located at 902 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the P-O-P vibration of P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> groups.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0036_R_d80179476e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span> Finally, the carbon content was calculated to be 10.0 wt % via TG analysis, as illustrated in Figure S9b. Carbon content of other samples with different content of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> are also shown in Figure S9. In addition, to ensure the effectiveness of the solid-solution strategy in achieving the pure-phase material in other mixed-anion-group compounds, Na<sub>4</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is partitioned into 2NaCoPO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ Na<sub>2</sub>CoP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and their corresponding XRD patterns with different content of NaCoPO<sub>4</sub> are compared in Figure S11 and S12. The phase evolution of (2-<i>x</i>) NaCoPO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ Na<sub>2</sub>CoP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> demonstrates similar tendency as Fe-based materials and a pure phase forms when the content of NaCoPO<sub>4</sub> is decreased to 1.4 with the molecular formula of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (hereafter denoted as Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>PP). The Rietveld refinement confirms the same <i>Pn2<sub>1</sub>a</i> symmetry with its Fe-counterpart and the existence of Co vacancy in Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>PP. The detailed structural information is shown in Figure S13, and the corresponding refined lattice parameters are listed in Table S3. Additionally, the carbon content of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>PP as well as other samples with content of NaCoPO<sub>4</sub> were calculated to be about 7.0 wt % via TG analysis, as shown in Figure S14. Moreover, as shown in Figure S15, the strategy is also effective in eliminating NaMnPO<sub>4</sub> impurity in the synthesis process of Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0037_R_d80179476e1448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202316957-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202316957-sec-0004-title"> Sodium storage performance</h3>
            
            <p>The cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of Na<sub>3.1</sub>Fe<sub>2.1</sub>PP, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP, and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>PP, were conducted with a sweep rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within the potential range of 1.5–4.3 V. Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2a</a> reveals that all three samples exhibit similar two redox pairs, namely 2.99/2.88 V and 3.26/3.18 V pairs. The lower redox pair is attributed to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion in orthorhombic phase Nr. 1 to form a new orthorhombic phase Nr. 2, while the higher one is mainly due to a solid-solution reaction in Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP with details shown in the subsequent in situ synchrotron discussion part. The potential plateau at ≈2.5 V shown in the Na<sub>3.1</sub>Fe<sub>2.1</sub>PP sample (the inset of Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2a</a>) is identified as the contribution from Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0015_R_d80179476e1498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> Furthermore, on subsequent cycles, nearly all of the CV profiles of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP overlap with each other, suggesting excellent electrochemical reversibility, as shown in Figure S16. As displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2b</a>, the Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP electrode records the highest discharge capacity of 110.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (1 C=121.0 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), which is significantly greater than that of Na<sub>3.1</sub>Fe<sub>2.1</sub>PP (103.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>PP (88.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). This improvement can be ascribed to the reduction in phase impurity, where both NaFePO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> can impair performance. Also, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP shows an exceptional rate performance, as displayed in Figures <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2c</a> and <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2d</a>, with a discharge capacity of 50.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at an extremely high rate of 100 C, proving a rapid charge-discharge cycle of 29 seconds. Such high-rate capability can be understood in terms of major contribution from pseudocapacitance (Figure S17). In comparison to the other two materials, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP displays the most impressive rate performance, highlighting the superiority of pure phase formation. In addition to its outstanding rate capabilities, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP exhibits an exceptionally long lifespan of over 14000 cycles, with high capacity retention of 83.1 % at 20 C and Coulombic efficiency of nearly 100 % (Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2e</a>), which is superior to all samples with impurity (shown in Figure S18 and Table S4). A slight carbon content discrepancy of these samples is proved to have very limited effect in the electrochemical properties of these samples, as shown in Figure S10. The superior kinetic property of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP is further estimated using Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) (as shown in Figure S19), which demonstrates an approximately one magnitude improvement in the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in comparison with Na<sub>3.1</sub>Fe<sub>2.1</sub>PP and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>PP. It is worth noting that the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP is comparable to NASICON-type compound<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0038_R_d80179476e1585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span> and significantly higher than that of maricite-NaFePO<sub>4</sub><span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0039_R_d80179476e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Power Sources">24</a></sup></span> and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub><span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0015_R_d80179476e1598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span> indicating a faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP material. Consequently, the pure phase material demonstrates superior rate performance compared to samples containing impurities. It is important to note that the advance in the electrochemical performances of pure-phase Na<sub>4</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> are also obvious that Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> demonstrates a higher capacity (Figure S20) and superior cycle performance (Figure S21) compared to the samples with impurity.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202316957-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/86941734-a2ef-43f0-bfb5-4b9b79ee8170/anie202316957-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/86941734-a2ef-43f0-bfb5-4b9b79ee8170/anie202316957-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03b62aaa-e07b-4ed8-bef0-782991c2c4eb/anie202316957-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/86941734-a2ef-43f0-bfb5-4b9b79ee8170/anie202316957-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202316957-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202316957" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) CV profiles of samples with different NaFePO<sub>4</sub> content, the inset is the enlarged image between 2.2 V and 2.7 V; (b) Charge-discharge profiles of samples during the second cycle at 0.1 C; (c) Comparison of rate capabilities of samples with different NaFePO<sub>4</sub> content; (d) Rate capability, and (e) long-term cycle stability of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP; (f) Discharging areal capacity, (g)  rate capability, and (h) cycling performance of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP/nanotube composite cathode as a function of mass loading.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The utilization of thick electrodes is a viable method for increasing the energy density of batteries via increasing the electrochemical active components per unit area. However, the processes of thick electrodes through traditional electrode formulations present a significant challenge due to cracking and poor rate performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0030_R_d80179476e1672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">17</a></sup></span> To address this issue, a composite of 1.0 wt % single-walled carbon nanotube (SWCNT) and 1.5 wt % CMC was utilized as conductive additive and binder to fabricate Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP thick electrodes. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2f</a>, the thickest electrode achieves a high mass loading of 41.1 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, much superior to the cathode fabricated by the traditional method (a mass loading of 1.4 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>). The areal capacity of the thickest Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP electrode increased linearly with the mass loading, reaching 3.7 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, yielding a specific capacity of 90.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when normalized to the total mass of the electrode (as shown in Figure S22). In contrast, the specific capacity of the traditional electrode only achieved 69.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The thickest electrode also delivers an excellent rate capability with an areal capacity of 1.9 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at a current density of 10.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2g</a>) in less than 11 minutes. In comparison, it is less than 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> for other electrodes prepared by slurry casting (Table S5). Additionally, the thick electrodes with various mass loadings demonstrate a stable discharging capacity over 50 cycles as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0002">2h</a>. Therefore, we have not only successfully fabricated high mass loading thick electrodes, but also attained superior rate performance, showing great promise for the next generation of high-energy-density batteries. Moreover, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP|hard carbon full cell was successfully assembled, exhibiting comparable capacity of 93.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (calculated by the mass of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP) to the half cell and excellent cycling stability of nearly 100 % over 100 cycles as shown in Figure S23.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202316957-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202316957-sec-0005-title"> Sodium storage mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the structural evolution of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP during electrochemical cycling, in situ synchrotron-based X-ray diffraction patterns were collected at the MSPD beamline at ALBA Synchrotron (Barcelona, Spain). The in situ diffraction patterns in the angular region were measured as a function of reaction time and voltage, as shown in Figures <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0003">3b</a>. Prior to cycling, all the reflections of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP exhibit an orthorhombic symmetry with space group <i>Pn2<sub>1</sub>a</i> (orthorhombic phase Nr. 1). As the voltage increased from open-circuit to 3.0 V, a new orthorhombic phase Nr. 2 emerges and grows at the expense of orthorhombic phase Nr. 1, resulting in a two-phase coexistence region that is consistent with the long voltage plateau at 3.0 V. The lattice parameters of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP do not change significantly during the long voltage plateau at ≈3.0 V (Figure S24), also indicating a two-phase transformation mechanism. Upon charging to 4.0 V, the position and intensity of reflections continuously change, indicative of a solid-solution reaction in Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP. The lattice parameters <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> decrease and increase, respectively, while lattice parameter <i>c</i> progressively increases, resulting in a reduction of the unit-cell volume of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP. At the end of charging (4.3 V), a set of new reflections assigned to an orthorhombic structure (orthorhombic phase Nr. 3) appears, signifying a typical two-phase transition mechanism and revealing the sluggish kinetics of the phase transformation at high voltage regions (&gt;4.2 V). The negligible difference in lattice parameter and unit-cell volume V of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP before and after charging, which is less than 1 %, highlights its exceptional reversibility and structural stability.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202316957-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/45a28f50-df42-4b50-9bb5-48d5f16efb9f/anie202316957-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/45a28f50-df42-4b50-9bb5-48d5f16efb9f/anie202316957-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/80f246cd-1359-4a29-b82d-8efeba132a7f/anie202316957-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/45a28f50-df42-4b50-9bb5-48d5f16efb9f/anie202316957-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202316957-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202316957" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>(a) Contour plot of in situ synchrotron-based XRD patterns of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP and corresponding charge-discharge voltage profile of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP cathode during the first cycle; (b) The corresponding 3D maps of intensity and positions of selected reflections for Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>In addition, an in situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) experiment was performed at the P64 beamline of PETRA-III, DESY (Germany). During the first cycle, the in situ X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP are obtained and depicted in Figures <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0004">4a</a> and <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0004">4b</a>. The Fe K-absorption edge of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP shifts to higher energy as the voltage approaches 4.3 V, indicating the oxidation of Fe(II) to Fe(III) (i.e., Fe<sup>2+</sup>→Fe<sup>3+</sup>+e<sup>−</sup>). Upon discharge, the XANES spectra demonstrate a tendency to shift back to lower energy, indicating the reduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup>. Notably, the shape and position of the XANES spectra do not return to their original state, implying an irreversible local structural change in Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP following the initial cycle, in agreement with the diffraction analysis. The extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) region of the XAS spectra is utilized to investigate the variation in the local environment of Fe ions in Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP. The Fourier transform (FT R-space) magnitudes of <i>k</i><sup>2</sup> weighted χ(<i>k</i>) EXAFS spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0004">4c</a>) displays two peaks at the Fe K-edge, representing the Fe-O interactions and Fe-P interactions in the orthorhombic structure. The intensity of the Fe-O peak gradually increases during charging, likely due to the formation of the orthorhombic phase Nr. 3.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202316957-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eea03417-5155-4375-916b-1680203c1626/anie202316957-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eea03417-5155-4375-916b-1680203c1626/anie202316957-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/083b724d-c707-46d9-a8fc-f77ab0eb328b/anie202316957-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eea03417-5155-4375-916b-1680203c1626/anie202316957-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202316957-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202316957" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Charge compensation mechanism of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP/nanotube composite cathode. (a–b) In situ XANES spectra at the Fe K-edge of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP electrode during the first cycle; (c) The Fourier transform magnitudes of <i>k</i><sup>2</sup> weighted Fe K-edge EXAFS spectra for Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP; (d) Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra for Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP electrodes at pristine and different charge-discharged states, only the isotropic signals are shown; (e) Local structural illustration of different sodium sites.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To investigate the structural evolution and sodium extraction sequence of cathodes during electrochemical cycling, ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na magic-angle-spinning (MAS) nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra were collected and analyzed for the Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP/nanotube composite cathodes at various charging and discharging states during the first cycle. The entire <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra of the cycled composite cathodes were presented in Figure S25, in which multiple spinning sidebands (SSBs) cover up to 1000 ppm for full width due to the strong intensive anisotropy. The centroid regions of the spectra are magnified in Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0004">4d</a>. The spectrum of the pristine cathode exhibits three resonances correspond to Na2 (100 ppm), Na1, Na4 (−6 ppm), and Na3 (−78 ppm) sites in the crystal structure (Figure <a href="#anie202316957-fig-0004">4e</a>), respectively. The assignment is based on the qualitative analysis of different Na-Fe bond lengths and Na-O-Fe bond angles, which are shown in Table S1 and determined from Rietveld refinement.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0028_R_d80179476e1918" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Power Sources">15</a></sup></span> The sodium atoms at Na2 and Na3 sites with more Na-O-Fe bonds (≈90°) exhibit a larger chemical shift, while Na1 and Na4 locate in a similar chemical shift region (≈0 ppm), this small hyperfine shifts is caused by the large inductive effect by polyanion PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202316957-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0019_R_d80179476e1924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">10</a>, <a href="#anie202316957-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202316957-bib-0040_R_d80179476e1924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Rev.">25</a></sup></span> Upon charging the electrode to 2.9 V, a new sharp peak at ≈7 ppm appears, revealing a new phase occurs for Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP. The signal of Na2 and Na3 is quickly weakened, indicating easier extraction during charge. When the cathode is continually charged to 3.2 V, the signal of Na2 and Na3 is further reduced due to deeper extraction of Na2 and Na3 ions. At 4.3 V, the signals of Na2 and Na3 disappear completely as expected for the full sodium extraction. The content of Na1 also decreases significantly. Upon the subsequent discharge process, the spectrum (Disch 1.5 V) reveals that only a portion of Na2 and Na3 are intercalated back into their original sites (Figure S26), while Na1 retains a higher intensity compared to the pristine cathode due to the incomplete reversibility of the phase transition revealed by XRD. The decrease of Na2/Na3 possibly originates from only partial Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation back upon discharge since the electrochemical cycling profile shows that the specific capacity of charge and discharge in the first circle is 122.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 109.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, the cathode discharged to 1.5 V displays a sharper signal, being a possible indicative of the increased Na ion mobility after one cycle, resulting in superior fast charging performance. The intensity and position of the Na4 peak remain unchanged throughout the electrochemical cycling, suggesting negligible alteration in the surrounding structure of Na4 and the valence state of iron, thereby highlighting imperfect solid-solution reaction during sodium extraction. And the signal of Na4 moves slightly to a low chemical shift direction, which may be attributed to the distortion of the local lattice caused by the phase transition after the electrochemical cycle, like the distance between inserted Na ion and the transition metal or oxygen ion changes. This observation underscores the stability of the structural framework during the electrochemical process, which could be the primary reason for the outstanding rate and fast charging performance of Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>PP.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202316957-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202316957-sec-0006-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We propose a solid-solution strategy to successfully synthesize a nonstoichiometric pure-phase Na<sub>3.4</sub>Fe<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, which also shows strong applicability in fabricating a Co-based mixed-anion-group compound, Na<sub>3.4</sub>Co<sub>2.4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>1.4</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. The resulting cathode exhibits impressive performance, including a high specific capacity of 110.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, ultrahigh rate capability of 100 C, and durable lives of 14000 cycles owing to the elimination of impurities. This study also reveals a two-phase transition that differs from previous reports and provides valuable insights into the synthesis and future application of mixed-anion-group Fe-based phosphate materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
